[5:2:344,499] oversight and control of an agency secretary.

[5:4:344,519] Legislature, Judicial Branch, Governor's Office,  

[5:5:344,529] and constitutional officers. This section also  

[5:6:344,539] includes such independent entities as the  

[6:0:344,549] Inspector General and the California State Lottery.

[1:0:268,43] 0110   Senate  

[2:0:66,64] The Legislature is comprised of two houses, the Senate and the Assembly, and is responsible for enacting laws and  

[2:1:66,74] establishing public policy for California. The State Senate is comprised of 40 members who are elected for 4-year terms.  

[2:2:66,84] Members of the Legislature may serve a maximum of 12 years total in the Senate, the Assembly, or both, in any combination of  

[2:3:66,95] terms. Members first elected before June 6, 2012, however, may generally serve a maximum of 14 years total, with no more  

[2:4:66,105] than two terms in the Senate and three terms in the Assembly.

[2:1:66,74] establishing public policy for California. The State Assembly is comprised of 80 members who are elected for 2-year terms.

[2:3:66,95] terms. Members first elected before June 6, 2012, however, may generally serve a maximum of 14 years total, with no more  

[2:4:66,105] than two terms in the Senate and three terms in the Assembly.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE   2019-20 STATE BUDGET — LJE   9 

[1:0:243,43] 0130   Joint Expenses  

[2:0:66,64] The Legislature is comprised of two houses, the Senate and the Assembly, and is responsible for enacting laws and  

[2:1:66,74] establishing public policy for California. Both houses contribute to support the Legislative Analyst's Office, which provides fiscal  

[2:2:66,84] and policy advice to the Legislature, and is overseen by the 16-member bipartisan Joint Legislative Budget Committee.  

[2:3:66,102] Because the Legislature's   programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Legislature has a capital outlay program to  

[2:4:66,112] support this need.   For the specifics on the Legislature's   capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."  

[3:0:66,143] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[11:0:66,531] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[12:0:66,549] The California State Capitol, which serves as the state’s working seat of government, began construction in 1860 and was  

[12:1:66,560] completed in 1874. The Capitol remained largely unchanged until 1949, when additional space requirements drove the need for  

[12:2:66,570] construction of the Capitol Annex, which attaches to the east side of the original Capitol building.   The Annex, which was  

[12:3:66,580] completed in 1951, houses legislative offices, committee rooms, and the Governor’s offices.  

[12:4:66,598] Government Code Section 9112(a) authorizes the Joint Rules Committee to pursue the construction of a new, restored,  

[12:5:66,608] rehabilitated, renovated, or reconstructed capitol building annex and associated projects.

[1:0:139,43] 0150   Contributions to the Legislators' Retirement System  

[1:1:66,64] Established in 1947, the Legislators' Retirement System provides retirement and death benefits for legislators, constitutional  

[1:2:66,74] officers, and legislative statutory officers.  

[2:0:66,95] The California Public Employees' Retirement System (CalPERS) administers the Legislators' Retirement System on behalf of  

[2:1:66,105] its members. Retirement benefits paid are based upon age, years of service, and final compensation. Benefits for death,  

[2:2:66,115] disability, and surviving dependents of deceased members are also provided under specific conditions.   The Legislators'  

[2:3:66,125] Retirement System is funded through contributions from the state, members of the system, and investment earnings. 

[3:0:66,146] Proposition 140, approved by voters in 1990, closed the Legislators' Retirement System to all new legislators after November  

[3:1:66,156] 6, 1990.   Subsequently, the California Public Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of 2012 (AB 340)  

[3:2:66,166] closed the Legislators' Retirement System to all new members effective January 1, 2013. New constitutional officers, statutory  

[3:3:66,176] officers, Sergeant-at-Arms, and the Insurance Commissioner retain optional membership rights in CalPERS.

[1:0:208,43] 0160   Legislative Counsel Bureau  

[2:0:66,64] The Legislative Counsel Bureau (Bureau) provides legal assistance to the two houses of the Legislature, and their members  

[2:1:66,74] and committees, by resolving a large volume of complex legal problems arising in connection with the legislative process. The  

[2:2:66,84] legal services furnished include rendering opinions, drafting bills, counseling, attending as counsel meetings of legislative  

[2:3:66,95] committees, and representing the Legislature in litigation. The attorney-client relationship is maintained and all work is  

[2:4:66,105] confidential.  

[3:0:66,125] In addition, the Bureau prepares and provides necessary indices and appropriate tables necessary to identify legislative  

[3:1:66,135] measures and compiles and indexes statutes and codes.  

[4:0:66,156] The Bureau operates the Legislative Data Center, which provides information technology services in support of the legislative  

[4:1:66,166] information system and the processing of legislative measures.  

[5:0:66,186] Pursuant to Chapter 46 of the Statutes of 2012, the California Commission on Uniform State Laws was transferred into the  

[5:1:66,197] Bureau. The California Commission on Uniform State Laws presents to the Legislature uniform laws recommended by the  

[5:2:66,207] National Conference of Commissioners on Uniform State Laws and then promotes passage of these uniform acts.  

[5:3:66,225] Pursuant to Chapter 450 of the Statutes of 2018, the Bureau provides advice and investigation services to the Legislature  

[5:4:66,235] related to workplace misconduct through the Workplace Conduct Unit.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE   2019-20 STATE BUDGET — LJE   17 

[1:0:242,43] 0250   Judicial Branch  

[2:0:66,64] Article VI of the Constitution creates the Supreme Court of California and the Courts of Appeal to exercise the judicial power of  

[2:1:66,74] the state at the appellate level. Article VI also creates the Judicial Council of California to administer the state's judicial system.  

[2:2:66,84] Chapter 869, Statutes of 1997, created the California Habeas Corpus Resource Center to represent any person financially  

[2:3:66,95] unable to employ appellate counsel in capital cases.  

[3:0:66,115] The Lockyer-Isenberg Trial Court Funding Act of 1997 (Chapter 850, Statutes of 1997) provided a stable and consistent  

[4:0:66,125] funding source for the trial courts. Beginning with fiscal year 1997-98, consolidation of the costs of trial court operation was  

[4:1:66,135] implemented at the state level, with the exception of facility, revenue collection, and local judicial benefit costs. This  

[5:0:66,146] implementation capped the counties' general purpose revenue contributions to trial court costs at a revised 1994-95 level. The  

[5:1:66,156] county contributions become part of the Trial Court Trust Fund, which supports all trial court operations. Fine and penalty  

[6:0:66,166] revenue collected by each county is retained or distributed in accordance with statute. Each county makes quarterly payments  

[6:1:66,176] to the Trial Court Trust Fund equal to the fine and penalty revenue received by the state General Fund in 1994-95, as adjusted  

[6:2:66,186] by amounts equivalent to specified fine and fee revenues that counties benefited from in 2003-04. The Trial Court Facilities Act  

[6:3:66,197] of 2002 (Chapter 1082, statutes of 2002) provided a process for the responsibility for court facilities to be transferred from the  

[6:4:66,207] counties to the state by July 1, 2007, which was extended to December 31, 2009, by Chapter 9, Statutes of 2008. The Trial  

[7:0:66,217] Court Facilities Act of 2002 also established several new revenue sources, effective on January 1, 2003. These revenues are  

[7:1:66,227] deposited into the State Court Facilities Construction Fund for the purpose of funding the construction and maintenance of  

[8:0:66,237] court facilities throughout the state. Counties contribute revenues for the ongoing operation and maintenance of court facilities  

[8:1:66,248] based upon historical expenditures for facilities transferred to the state.  

[9:0:66,268] The mission of the Judicial Branch is to resolve disputes arising under the law and to interpret and apply the law consistently,  

[9:1:66,278] impartially, and independently to protect the rights and liberties guaranteed by the Constitutions of California and the United  

[9:2:66,288] States, in a fair, accessible, effective, and efficient manner.  

[10:0:66,309] Because the Judicial Branch's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Judicial Branch has a capital outlay  

[10:1:66,319] program to support this need. For additional information regarding the Judicial Branch's capital outlay program, see  

[10:2:66,329] "Infrastructure Overview."  

[11:0:66,360] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS  

[12:0:318,381] Positions Expenditures  

[12:1:283,394] 2017-18 2018-19 2019-20 2017-18* 2018-19* 2019-20*

[13:2:75,432] 0140 Judicial Council 687.3 710.6 731.1 614,739 662,743 676,768  

[13:3:75,445] 0150 State Trial Court Funding - - - 2,755,332 2,952,899 3,288,447

[11:0:66,295] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[12:0:65,313] • Pretrial Pilot Projects—The Budget includes $75 million General Fund to be allocated over a two-year period by the Judicial  

[12:1:76,323] Council for the implementation, operation, and evaluation of programs or efforts in at least 10 courts related to pretrial  

[12:2:76,333] decision-making.  

[13:0:65,349] • Superior Court Judgeships—The Budget includes $30.4 million General Fund in 2019-20 and $36.5 million General Fund  

[13:1:76,360] annually thereafter for 25 additional superior court judgeships.  

[14:0:65,376] • Trial Court Case Management System Replacements—The Budget includes $23.1 million General Fund in 2019-20 and  

[14:1:76,386] $6.7 million in 2020-21 to replace 14 case management systems in 10 trial courts.  

[15:0:65,402] • Facilities, Operations and Maintenance—The Budget includes $20.2 million ongoing General Fund to support operations and  

[15:1:76,412] maintenance of trial court facilities constructed since 2007, and $15 million one-time for deferred maintenance projects.  

[15:2:65,429] • Dependency Counsel—The Budget includes $20 million ongoing General Fund for court-appointed dependency counsel that  

[15:3:76,439] represent children and parents at every stage of dependency proceedings. Additionally, the recent federal Families First  

[15:4:76,449] Prevention Services Act expanded the list of eligible Title IV-E reimbursable activities to include court-appointed dependency  

[15:5:76,459] counsel costs. The Budget includes $1.5 million ongoing General Fund for the Judicial Council to administer these federal  

[15:6:76,469] reimbursements, which are estimated to be $34 million annually.  

[16:0:65,486] • Legal Aid for Renters in Landlord-Tenant Disputes—The Budget includes $20 million one-time General Fund to provide legal  

[16:1:76,496] aid for renters in landlord-tenant disputes. Specifically, these additional funds will provide free legal services for landlord-

[16:2:76,506] tenant issues, including legal assistance for counseling, renter education programs, and preventing evictions.  

[16:3:65,522] • Cannabis Conviction Resentencing Workload, Chapter 993, Statutes of 2018 (AB 1793)—The Budget includes $13.9 million  

[16:4:76,532] General Fund in 2019-20 and $2.9 million in 2020-21 for anticipated resentencing workload associated with Chapter 993,  

[16:5:76,543] Statutes of 2018 (AB 1793), which expedites the identification, review, and notification of individuals who may be eligible for  

[16:6:76,553] recall or dismissal, dismissal or sealing, or redesignation of the conviction for specified cannabis-related offenses.  

[16:7:65,569] • Language Access Expansion in the California Courts—The Budget includes $13.6 million ongoing General Fund to continue  

[16:8:76,579] the expansion of interpreter services for civil matters and to cover increased costs in criminal cases.  

[16:9:65,595] • Compensation for State-Level Judiciary Staff—The Budget includes $9.5 million ongoing General Fund for a general salary  

[16:10:76,606] increase for state-level judiciary employees at the Judicial Council, California Supreme Court, and Appellate Courts.  

[16:11:65,622] • Improvement and Modernization Fund Stabilization—The Budget includes $8.8 million ongoing to shift existing programs to  

[16:12:76,632] the General Fund to maintain solvency in the Improvement and Modernization Fund. Specifically, the Budget shifts $5.6  

[16:13:76,642] million for the Litigation Management Program and $3.2 million for the Personnel Management System.  

[16:14:65,658] • Trial Court Security Systems and Equipment—The Budget includes $6 million ongoing General Fund to refresh, maintain,  

[16:15:76,669] and replace trial court security equipment and systems.  

[17:0:65,685] • Digitizing Court Records—The Budget includes $5.6 million one-time General Fund to establish a pilot program to digitize  

[17:1:76,695] paper and film case files at several trial courts.  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:65,69] • Business Intelligence and Data Analytics—The Budget includes $5.5 million one-time General Fund to establish and support  

[3:1:76,79] the foundation of a business intelligence, data analytics, and identity and access management program to improve court  

[3:2:76,89] efficiencies and service to the public.  

[4:0:65,105] • Courts of Appeal Workload—The Budget includes $5 million ongoing General Fund to address general operational cost  

[4:1:76,115] increases, workload due to more complex litigation, new case duties related to recent law changes requiring retroactive  

[4:2:76,126] decisions related to criminal justice reforms, and voter approved initiatives requiring appellate review.  

[4:3:65,142] • Implementation and Maintenance of Personnel Management Systems—The Budget includes $4.5 million ongoing General  

[4:4:76,152] Fund to complete the first step in migrating to an updated personnel system, also known as Phoenix, and to roll out the  

[4:5:76,162] system at seven additional trial courts.  

[5:0:65,178] • Shriver Act Civil Counsel—The Budget includes $2.5 million one-time General Fund for the expansion and administration of  

[5:1:76,189] pilot programs that provide representation to low-income Californians on critical legal issues affecting basic human needs.  

[5:2:65,205] • Trial Court Employee Benefits—Beginning in 2019-20, the Budget includes a mechanism to fund trial court employee  

[5:3:76,215] benefits in the current year. The Budget includes $22.5 million General Fund for recent trial court employee benefits, which  

[5:4:76,225] brings the total funding provided by the state to approximately $150.5 million for these increased costs.  

[5:5:65,241] • Trial Court Trust Fund Revenue Backfill—The Budget continues funding commitments made by the state to backfill the Trial  

[5:6:76,252] Court Trust Fund as a result of declining fine and penalty revenues. The Budget includes a total of $41.8 million General  

[5:7:76,262] Fund to backfill the decline of fines and penalty revenues expected in 2019-20.

[24:0:82,606] 4/ Reflects pending allocations from the Judicial Council. Courts may have budgeted for some of these funds in section 1 in anticipation of their allocation.
[25:0:82,613] 5/ See the "State Trial Court Funding Not Distributed to the Trial Courts" display for more detail. Funds either disbursed on behalf of trial courts, for statewide programs, for judges compensation, or to non-court entities, and as a result are not included in the superior courts’  

[25:1:82,619] state funding in Section 1. 

[26:0:82,625] 6/ See the "State Trial Court Funding Not Distributed to the Trial Courts" display for more detail. These expenses are funded by the trial courts opting to participate in those programs from their "State Funding" revenue distribution amounts reported in Section 1 of the "State and  

[26:1:82,631] Non-State Trial Court Funding, Expenditures, and Positions" display. Because the funding for these expenses is already included in Section 1, they are subtracted from the total "State Trial Court Funding Not Distributed to Trial Courts" amount used to calculate the total  

[26:2:82,637] expenditures displayed for "State Trial Court Funding Total". 

[27:0:82,642] 7/ For the prior year, recognizes that the revenues courts report individually as received or to be received from the state will not be equal to the "State Trial Court Funding" expenditure amount reported at the state level due to timing and accounting differences. For the current  

[27:1:82,648] year, this category reflects either differences in local versus state revenue projections, pending court budgets, or unallocated appropriation. 

[28:0:82,654] 8/ Total state funding for trial courts ties to actual and accrued expenditures for 2017-18 and estimated expenditures for 2018-19 for Program 0150 "State Trial Court Funding" included in the 2019-20 Enacted Budget.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:414,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — LJE   25 

[2:0:207,32] 0250   Judicial Branch - Continued 

[3:0:66,76] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,94] 0130 - SUPREME COURT  

[4:0:66,115] The Supreme Court is the highest court in the California judicial system. Its decisions are binding on all other California state  

[4:1:66,125] courts. The Chief Justice of California and the six Associate Justices entertain petitions seeking review of decisions from the  

[4:2:66,135] Courts of Appeal, original petitions for extraordinary relief (such as writs of mandate or habeas corpus), and recommendations  

[4:3:66,145] for discipline of judicial officers and attorneys. The Court grants review and issues opinions in order to settle legal questions of  

[4:4:66,156] statewide importance. In addition, under the California Constitution, all death penalty judgments are appealed directly to the  

[4:5:66,166] Supreme Court.  

[4:6:66,184] 0135 - COURTS OF APPEAL  

[5:0:66,204] Established by a constitutional amendment in 1904, the Courts of Appeal are California's intermediate courts of review. The six  

[5:1:66,214] District Courts of Appeal hear appeals and original proceedings at nine different locations around the state. Cases before the  

[5:2:66,224] Courts of Appeal involve every area of civil and criminal law.  

[5:3:66,242] 0140 - JUDICIAL COUNCIL  

[6:0:66,262] The Judicial Council of California is the constitutional policy-making body for the state judiciary. The Council consists of 21  

[6:1:66,273] voting members and 11 advisory members; the Chief Justice of California serves as chair. The Judicial Council staff serve as  

[6:2:66,283] the administrative arm of the Council. Staff provide policy support to the Council, administrative accountability in the operation  

[6:3:66,293] of the courts as specified by law, strategic planning for capital outlay, design, and construction of court facilities; and  

[6:4:66,303] administrative support for courts in areas such as budget, fiscal services, coordination of the assignment of retired judges,  

[6:5:66,313] technology, education, legal advice and services, human resources, legislative advocacy, and research.  

[6:6:66,331] 0140023 - JUDICIAL BRANCH FACILITY PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,349] The Judicial Branch Facility Program administers the acquisition, planning, construction, operations, and maintenance of  

[7:1:66,359] judicial branch facilities. This program is responsible for the development of long-term facilities master plans, facility and real  

[7:2:66,369] estate management, renovation of existing court facilities, and new courthouse planning, design, and construction.  

[7:3:66,387] 0150 - STATE TRIAL COURT FUNDING  

[7:4:66,405] 0150010 - SUPPORT FOR THE OPERATION OF THE TRIAL COURTS  

[8:0:66,422] This program's objective is to provide the resources necessary for the statewide trial court system to adjudicate civil and  

[8:1:66,433] criminal cases. This program includes all allowable trial court administrative costs under Government Code section 77003,  

[8:2:66,443] except court-appointed dependency counsel, salaries and benefits of Superior Court judges, compensation for assigned  

[8:3:66,453] judges, support for language interpreters, grants, and expenses on behalf of the trial courts.  

[8:4:66,471] 0150011 - COURT APPOINTED DEPENDENCY COUNSEL  

[9:0:66,488] This program provides funding for court-appointed counsel for children, non-minor dependents and parents in juvenile court  

[9:1:66,499] dependency proceedings pursuant to Welfare and Institutions Code section 317, subdivisions (b) and (c), and Government  

[9:2:66,509] Code section 77003, subdivision (a)(4).  

[9:3:66,526] 0150019 - COMPENSATION OF SUPERIOR COURT JUDGES  

[9:4:66,544] This program provides funding for the salaries and state benefits for Superior Court judges.  

[9:5:66,562] 0150028 - ASSIGNED JUDGES  

[10:0:66,580] This program provides support for the salaries and related costs of retired as well as active judges who are assigned by the  

[10:1:66,590] Chief Justice to positions in courts which require assistance due to judicial absences or other factors impacting the ability of a  

[10:2:66,600] court to avoid case delay.  

[10:3:66,618] 0150037 - COURT INTERPRETERS  

[11:0:66,635] This program supports the provision of qualified language interpreters in criminal, juvenile, family and civil proceedings as  

[11:1:66,646] required by statute.  

[11:2:66,663] 0150046 - GRANTS  

[12:0:66,681] This program provides funding for government grants providing public services related to the trial courts.  

[12:1:66,699] 0150095 - EXPENSES ON BEHALF OF THE TRIAL COURTS  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — LJE   26 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:207,32] 0250   Judicial Branch - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] This program provides appropriation for statewide programs and services provided to the court or courts by the Judicial Council  

[3:1:66,79] or payment for services or property of any kind contracted for the court or courts or on behalf of the courts by the Judicial  

[3:2:66,89] Council, which are paid for or reimbursed by the trial courts. The appropriation also supports the implementation and  

[3:3:66,99] administration of the Civil Representation Pilot Program.  

[3:4:66,117] 0155 - HABEAS CORPUS RESOURCE CENTER  

[4:0:66,135] The Habeas Corpus Resource Center provides legal representation for indigent petitioners in death penalty habeas corpus  

[4:1:66,145] proceedings before the Supreme Court of California and the federal courts. The Center also recruits and trains attorneys to  

[4:2:66,155] expand the pool of private counsel qualified to accept appointments in death penalty habeas corpus proceedings, serves as a  

[4:3:66,165] resource to them, and thereby helps to reduce the number of unrepresented indigents on California's death row.

[5:0:66,159] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[6:0:66,177] The Judicial Branch consists of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, trial courts, and the Judicial Council. The Supreme Court  

[6:1:66,188] is located in the Civic Center Plaza in San Francisco (101,500 square feet) and the Ronald Reagan State Building in Los  

[6:2:66,198] Angeles (7,600 sf). The courts of appeal are organized into six districts, operate in nine different locations, and consist of  

[6:3:66,208] approximately 508,000 sf. The trial courts are located in 58 counties statewide, in approximately 500 buildings and 2,100  

[6:4:66,218] courtrooms of approximately 16 million sf of usable area and more than 21 million sf of space under Judicial Council  

[6:5:66,228] responsibility and management. The facilities of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, and trial courts encompass not only the  

[6:6:66,239] public courtroom spaces but also the chambers and workspaces where judicial officers and courtroom staff prepare for  

[6:7:66,249] proceedings; secure areas, including holding cells; and building support functions. Judicial Council administrative facilities,  

[6:8:66,259] occupying approximately 260,000 sf, are located in San Francisco and Sacramento.

[1:0:180,43] 0280   Commission on Judicial Performance  

[2:0:66,64] The California Commission on Judicial Performance is the independent state agency responsible for investigating complaints of  

[2:1:66,74] judicial misconduct and judicial incapacity, and for disciplining judges pursuant to Article VI, Section 18 of the California  

[2:2:66,84] Constitution. It is the only body with such authority in the state. Its jurisdiction includes all active judges and justices of  

[2:3:66,95] California's superior courts, Courts of Appeal and Supreme Court, and former judges for conduct prior to retirement or  

[2:4:66,105] resignation. The Commission also shares authority with the local courts for the oversight of court commissioners and referees.  

[2:5:66,115] In addition to its disciplinary functions, the Commission is responsible for handling judges' applications for disability retirement.  

[3:0:66,135] The Commission's authority is limited to investigating alleged judicial misconduct and, if warranted, imposing discipline. Judicial  

[3:1:66,146] misconduct usually involves conduct in conflict with the standards set forth in the Code of Judicial Ethics. After investigation  

[3:2:66,156] and, in some cases a public hearing, the Commission may impose sanctions ranging from confidential discipline to removal  

[3:3:66,166] from office.  

[4:0:66,186] The Commission is composed of 11 members: 3 judges appointed by the Supreme Court; 2 attorneys appointed by the  

[4:1:66,197] Governor; and 6 lay citizens, of which 2 are appointed by the Governor, 2 are appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules,  

[4:2:66,207] and 2 are appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly. Members are appointed to four-year terms and may serve two terms.  

[4:3:66,217] Commission members do not receive a salary.

[0:0:66,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — LJE   44   LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:150,43] 0390   Contributions to the Judges' Retirement System  

[2:0:66,64] The Judges' Retirement System provides retirement benefit funding for judges on California's Supreme, Appellate, and  

[2:1:66,74] Superior/Municipal courts. The California Public Employees' Retirement System administers both the Judges' Retirement  

[2:2:66,84] System and the Judges' Retirement System II on behalf of its members.  

[3:0:66,102] The Judges' Retirement System is closed to new members, and a second retirement system for judges was established in  

[3:1:66,112] 1994. All new judges elected or appointed on or after November 9, 1994 become members of the Judges' Retirement System  

[3:2:66,123] II. The Judges' Retirement System II provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service, final  

[3:3:66,133] compensation, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement System II Law. Judges' Retirement  

[3:4:66,143] System II members eligible for a service retirement have the option of choosing the monetary credit plan (a lump-sum return of  

[3:5:66,153] contributions and interest earned) or the defined benefit plan. Judges' Retirement System II members not eligible for a defined  

[3:6:66,163] benefit receive the balance of their monetary credits (in a lump-sum payment). The Judges' Retirement System II receives  

[3:7:66,174] contributions from judges equal to 8 percent of salary as well as any investment income. The state's contributions are adjusted  

[3:8:66,184] annually to maintain actuarial soundness of the fund.  

[4:0:66,204] The Judges' Retirement System II provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service,  

[4:1:66,214] compensation of active judges, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement Law. Active judges  

[4:2:66,225] and the state each contribute 8 percent of salary to the Judges' Retirement System. Additional contributions come from filing  

[4:3:66,235] fees for specific civil cases and investment income. These contributions, however, are not sufficient to fully fund benefit  

[4:4:66,245] payments. Consequently, current law requires the state to fund the difference between existing contribution sources and the  

[4:5:66,255] required benefit payments to retired judges.  

[5:0:66,276] Effective January 1, 2013, provisions of the California Public Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of  

[5:1:66,286] 2012 (AB 340), require that new judges contribute 50 percent of the normal cost of their pension benefits.

[5:0:66,324] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,343] 0220 - GO-Biz  

[6:0:66,363] The GO-Biz serves as the Governor's lead entity for economic strategy and the marketing of California on issues relating to  

[6:1:66,373] business development, private sector investment, and economic growth, and export promotion. This program makes  

[6:2:66,383] recommendations to the Governor and the Legislature regarding policies, programs, and actions to advance statewide  

[6:3:66,394] economic goals.  

[7:0:66,411] 0225 - CALIFORNIA BUSINESS INVESTMENT SERVICES  

[8:0:66,429] This program serves employers, corporate executives, business owners, and site location consultants who are considering  

[8:1:66,439] California for business investment and expansion. This program convenes teams on key business development issues. This  

[8:2:66,449] program works with local, state, and federal partners to attract, retain, and grow businesses in addition to providing permit  

[8:3:66,460] assistance and helping businesses succeed in California. The Innovation Hub (iHub) initiative improves the state's national and  

[8:4:66,470] global competitiveness by stimulating partnerships, economic development, and job creation around specific research clusters  

[8:5:66,480] through state-designated iHubs.  

[9:0:66,498] 0230 - OFFICE OF THE SMALL BUSINESS ADVOCATE  

[10:0:66,515] The director of the Office of the Small Business Advocate (OSBA) serves as the principal advocate in the state on behalf of  

[10:1:66,526] small businesses, including legislation and administrative regulations that affect small business. The OSBA is responsible for  

[10:2:66,536] disseminating information about programs and services provided by the state that benefit small businesses, and how small  

[10:3:66,546] businesses can participate in these programs and services. The OSBA responds to issues from small businesses concerning  

[10:4:66,556] the actions of state agencies, state laws and regulations adversely affecting those businesses. The OSBA maintains and  

[10:5:66,566] distributes an annual list of persons serving as small business ombudsmen throughout state government.  

[10:6:66,584] 0235010 - CALIFORNIA FILM COMMISSION  

[11:0:66,602] The California Film Commission (CFC) enhances California's status as the leader in motion picture, television and commercial  

[11:1:66,612] production. The Commission is tasked with retaining and increasing motion picture production and to see that it continues to  

[11:2:66,622] create jobs and boost business throughout the State. A one-stop office for filmmakers, the Commission supports productions of  

[11:3:66,632] all sizes and budgets with a variety of services. In addition to issuing film permits for all state properties, administering the film  

[11:4:66,643] and TV tax credit program, maintaining an extensive location library, and offering production assistance on a wide variety of  

[11:5:66,653] issues, CFC also works closely with cities and counties with the goal of creating 'film friendly" policies that are consistent state-

[11:6:66,663] wide.  

[12:0:66,681] 0235019 - DIVISION OF TOURISM  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:89,32] 0509   Governor's Office of Business and Economic Development (GO-Biz) - 

[2:1:276,47] Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The California Tourism Market Act provides for the marketing of California through an assessment of businesses that benefit  

[3:1:66,79] from travel and tourism. The objective of the Tourism Assessment Program is to identify potentially assessable businesses,  

[3:2:66,89] assist companies with determining the appropriate amount of their self-assessment, and collet the fee.  

[3:3:66,107] 0235028 - CALIFORINIA INFRASTRUCTURE AND ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENT BANK  

[4:0:66,124] California Infrastructure and Economic Development Bank (IBank) was created to finance public infrastructure and private  

[4:1:66,135] development that promote a healthy climate for jobs, contribute to a strong economy and improve the quality of life in California  

[4:2:66,145] communities. IBank has a broad authority to issue tax-exempt and taxable revenue bonds, provide financing to public  

[4:3:66,155] agencies, provide credit enhancements, acquire or lease facilities, and leverage State and Federal funds. IBank's current  

[4:4:66,165] programs include the infrastructure state revolving fund, 501(c)(3) tax-exempt and taxable revenue bond program, industrial  

[4:5:66,175] development revenue bond program, exempt facility revenue bond program, governmental bond program and the Clean  

[4:6:66,186] Energy Finance Center (CEFC) and the Statewide Energy Efficiency Program under the CEFC.  

[4:7:66,203] 0235037 - SMALL BUSINESS LOAN GUARANTEE PROGRAM  

[5:0:66,221] The Small Business Loan Guarantee Program (SBLGP) promotes local economic development by providing guarantees for  

[5:1:66,231] loans issued to small businesses from financial institutions, typically banks, that otherwise would not approve a term loan or  

[5:2:66,241] line of credit to a small business. As a result of the SBLGP, participating small businesses are able to secure financing that  

[5:3:66,252] allows them to grow and expand their business. The loan guarantee serves as a credit enhancement and an incentive for  

[5:4:66,262] financial institutions to make loans to small businesses that otherwise would not be eligible for such financing.  

[5:5:66,280] 0235046 - CALIFORNIA WELCOME CENTERS  

[6:0:66,297] California Welcome Centers are visitor information centers that are readily accessible to and recognizable by tourists to  

[6:1:66,307] encourage tourism in California and provide benefits to the state economy. The objective of the California Welcome Center  

[6:2:66,318] Program is to determine the locality of underserved travelers, designate a welcome center, and establish operating standards  

[6:3:66,328] across the network.  

[7:0:66,346] 0240 - COMMUNITY REINVESTMENT GRANT PROGRAM 

[8:0:66,363] The California Community Reinvestment Grants Program (CalCRG) was established on July 1, 2018   to   implement the  

[8:1:66,373] competitive grant program mandated by Proposition 64, the Adult Use of Marijuana Act, and the provisions specified in  

[8:2:66,384] Revenue and Taxation Code section 34019(d).   The CalCRG administers grants   for   local public   health departments and  

[8:3:66,394] qualified community-based nonprofit organizations to support various mental health treatment, substance use treatment, job  

[8:4:66,404] placement, legal services and other health and wellness-related programs for communities disproportionately affected by past  

[8:5:66,414] federal and state drug policies in California. GO-Biz must award grants annually and at least 50 percent of the grant funding will  

[8:6:66,424] be allocated to qualified community-based nonprofit organizations.

[1:0:157,43] 0511   Secretary for Government Operations Agency  

[2:0:66,64] The Government Operations Agency is responsible for coordinating state operations, including procurement, information  

[2:1:66,74] technology, and human resources. The mission of the Government Operations Agency is to improve management and  

[2:2:66,84] accountability of government programs, increase efficiency, and promote better and more coordinated operations decisions.  

[2:3:66,95] The Government Operations Agency oversees the Department of General Services, Department of Human Resources,  

[2:4:66,105] Department of Technology, Franchise Tax Board, State Personnel Board, Victim Compensation Board, Office of Administrative  

[2:5:66,115] Law, California Public Employees' Retirement System, and California State Teachers' Retirement System.   Beginning July 1,  

[2:6:66,125] 2018, the Government Operations Agency assumed oversight responsibility for the California Complete Count Census  

[2:7:66,135] Program.   The Census Program will be an extensive statewide outreach and awareness campaign designed to encourage and  

[2:8:66,146] support full participation by all Californians in the upcoming 2020 Census.   Lastly, beginning July 1, 2019, the Government  

[2:9:66,156] Operations Agency will administer the newly established Office of Digital Innovation, whose primary focus is   the   efficient  

[2:10:66,166] delivery of government services.

[1:0:99,43] 0515   Secretary for Business, Consumer Services, and Housing Agency

[3:0:66,74] commissions and agencies that provide the following services to the people of California: protection of the public through the  

[3:1:66,84] licensing and regulation of more than 100 businesses and 200 professional categories; regulation of financial services and  

[3:2:66,95] state-licensed financial institutions; enforcement of the sale of alcoholic beverages; regulation of the horse racing industry;  

[3:3:66,105] protection of civil rights; safe and affordable housing opportunities; and earthquake safety. The Agency is comprised of the

[3:6:66,135] Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control, the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, the California Housing Finance  

[3:7:66,146] Agency, the California Horse Racing Board, the Alfred E. Alquist Seismic Safety Commission, and the Cannabis Control  

[3:8:66,156] Appeals Panel. In addition, the Agency is charged with administration of the Homeless Coordinating and Financing Council.

[5:0:66,137] The California Traffic Safety Program develops the California Highway Safety Plan. The plan uses available state and federal  

[5:1:66,148] resources to identify and address major traffic safety problems throughout the state.  

[5:2:66,165] 0276 - TRANSIT AND INTERCITY RAIL CAPITAL PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,186] The Transit and Intercity Rail Capital Program provides grants to encourage low carbon transit operations through the Transit,  

[6:1:66,196] Affordable Housing, and Sustainable Communities Program. The program goals include reducing greenhouse gas emissions,  

[6:2:66,206] improving mobility access across the state, and providing benefits to California's disadvantaged communities.

[1:0:117,43] 0530   Secretary for California Health and Human Services Agency  

[1:1:66,64] The primary mission of the Health and Human Services Agency (HHSA) is to provide policy leadership and direction to the  

[1:2:66,74] departments, board and programs it oversees, to reduce duplication and fragmentation among HHSA departments in policy  

[1:3:66,84] development and implementation, to improve coordination among departments on common programs, to ensure programmatic  

[1:4:66,95] integrity, and to advance the Governor's priorities on health and human services issues.  

[2:0:66,115] The HHSA accomplishes its mission through the administration and coordination of state and federal programs for public  

[2:1:66,125] health, health care services, social services, public assistance, health planning and licensing, and rehabilitation. These  

[2:2:66,135] programs touch the lives of millions of California's most needy and vulnerable residents. The HHSA is committed to striking a  

[2:3:66,146] balance between maintaining access to essential health and human services for California's most disadvantaged and at-risk  

[2:4:66,156] residents while pursuing ways to better manage and control costs.  

[2:5:66,176] The following departments and entities fall under the purview of the HHSA:  

[2:6:65,194] • Department of Aging  

[3:0:65,210] • Department of Child Support Services

[6:5:76,232] master plan to improve the state’s early learning and care system.  

[6:6:65,248] • The Budget includes one-time $5 million to create the Healthy California for All Commission, which will analyze California’s  

[6:7:76,258] existing health care delivery system and design options for a unified health care financing system, including but not limited to  

[6:8:76,268] a single payer system.  

[7:0:65,285] • The Budget includes ongoing $2.5 million General Fund to establish the Office of the Surgeon General, and fund the  

[7:1:76,295] appointments of a Deputy Secretary for Early Childhood Initiatives and Deputy Secretary for Behavioral Health.  

[7:2:65,311] • The Budget includes $640,000 General Fund in 2019-20 and $603,000 General Fund annually thereafter for the  

[7:3:76,321] reorganization of the Office of the Secretary.

[5:0:66,180] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,198] 0280 - SECRETARY FOR HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES  

[6:0:66,216] The Secretary for California Health and Human Services Agency provides the Governor with the highest level of advice on  

[6:1:66,226] state health, human services and related budget policy issues. The Secretary also provides the highest level of leadership and  

[6:2:66,236] oversight of the agency-wide efforts to promote the health and well-being of a growing and increasingly diverse California  

[6:3:66,246] population.  

[7:0:66,264] 0285 - THE CALIFORNIA OFFICE OF HEALTH INFORMATION INTEGRITY  

[8:0:66,282] The California Office of Health Information Integrity (CalOHII) is responsible for statewide leadership, coordination, policy  

[8:1:66,292] formation, direction, and oversight responsibilities for federal Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA)  

[8:2:66,302] requirements. As the leading resource for providing HIPAA expertise to the State of California, CalOHII exercises full authority  

[8:3:66,312] relative to state entities to establish policy, provide direction to state entities, monitor progress and report on implementation  

[8:4:66,322] efforts based on state and federal laws.  

[8:5:66,340] 0290 - OFFICE OF SYSTEMS INTEGRATION  

[9:0:66,361] This Office provides project management services for automation projects for the Health and Human Services Agency, the  

[9:1:66,371] Department of Social Services, the Department of Health Care Services, the Department of Public Health, and the Health  

[9:2:66,381] Benefit Exchange, including:  

[9:3:65,399] • Appeals Case Management System  

[10:0:65,415] • California Healthcare Eligibility, Enrollment and Retention System Project.  

[10:1:65,431] • Case Management, Information and Payrolling System II  

[10:2:65,447] • Child Welfare Services/Case Management System  

[10:3:65,463] • Child Welfare Services - New System  

[10:4:65,480] • Electronic Benefit Transfer System  

[11:0:65,496] • Electronic Women, Infants and Children Management Information System Project  

[11:1:65,512] • Medi-Cal Eligibility Data System Modernization  

[11:2:65,528] • Statewide Automated Welfare System  

[11:3:65,544] • Welfare Data Tracking Implementation Project  

[11:4:66,562] 0295 - OFFICE OF THE PATIENT ADVOCATE  

[12:0:66,580] The mission of the Office of the Patient Advocate is to improve the access to and quality of health care services by promoting  

[12:1:66,590] transparency and accountability. The Office of the Patient Advocate is mandated to produce a number of public reports on  

[12:2:66,600] health care service topics. Current reporting activities include:  

[12:3:65,618] • Online Annual Health Care Quality Report Cards on health plans and medical groups,  

[13:0:65,634] • A Baseline Review and annual report on the State Consumer Assistance Call Centers and health care complaint data,  

[13:1:65,650] • Online Timely Access Compliance report on California Department of Managed Health Care health care services plans and  

[13:2:76,661] providers, and  

[14:0:65,677] • Model protocols for State Consumer Assistance Call Centers.

[1:0:164,43] 0540   Secretary of the Natural Resources Agency  

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the Natural Resources Agency is to restore, protect and manage the state's natural, historical and cultural  

[2:1:66,74] resources for current and future generations using creative approaches and solutions based on science, collaboration and  

[2:2:66,84] respect for all involved communities. The secretary for Natural Resources, a member of the Governor's Cabinet, sets the  

[2:3:66,95] policies and coordinates the environmental preservation and restoration activities of 26 various departments, boards,  

[3:0:66,105] commissions and conservancies, and directly administers the Sea Grant Program, Ocean Protection Council, California  

[3:1:66,115] Environmental Quality Act, Environmental Enhancement Mitigation Program, River Parkways, Urban Greening, and the  

[3:2:66,125] California Cultural and Historical Endowment grant programs.  

[4:0:66,146] The Natural Resources Agency consists of the departments of Forestry and Fire Protection, Conservation, Fish and Wildlife,  

[4:1:66,156] Parks and Recreation, and Water Resources; the California Conservation Corps; Exposition Park; California Science Center;  

[4:2:66,166] California African American Museum; the State Lands Commission; the Colorado River Board; the San Francisco Bay  

[5:0:66,176] Conservation and Development Commission; the Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission; the Wildlife  

[5:1:66,186] Conservation Board; the Delta Protection Commission; the California Coastal Commission; the State Coastal Conservancy; the  

[5:2:66,197] California Tahoe Conservancy; the Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy; the Coachella Valley Mountains Conservancy; the  

[5:3:66,207] San Joaquin River Conservancy; the San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy; the Baldwin  

[5:4:66,217] Hills Conservancy; the San Diego River Conservancy; the Sierra Nevada Conservancy; the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta  

[5:5:66,227] Conservancy; the Native American Heritage Commission; and the Special Resources Program.

[0:0:66,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — LJE   94   LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:199,43] 0552   Office of the Inspector General  

[2:0:66,64] The Office of the Inspector General (OIG) protects public safety by safeguarding the integrity of California's correctional  

[2:1:66,74] system. OIG is responsible for contemporaneous oversight of the California Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation's  

[2:2:66,84] (CDCR) internal affairs investigations, use of force, and the employee disciplinary process. When requested by the Governor,  

[2:3:66,95] the Senate Committee on Rules, or the Speaker of the Assembly, the Inspector General reviews the policies, practices, and  

[2:4:66,105] procedures of CDCR. The Inspector General reviews the Governor's candidates for appointment to serve as warden for the  

[2:5:66,115] state's adult correctional institutions and as superintendents for the state's juvenile facilities, and   conducts metric-oriented  

[2:6:66,125] inspection programs to periodically review delivery of medical care at each state prison. OIG receives communications from  

[2:7:66,135] individuals alleging improper governmental activity and maintains a toll-free public telephone number to receive allegations of  

[2:8:66,146] wrongdoing by employees of CDCR;   conducts formal reviews of complaints of retaliation from CDCR employees against upper  

[2:9:66,156] management where a legally cognizable cause of action is present; and reviews the mishandling of sexual abuse incidents  

[2:10:66,166] within correctional institutions. OIG provides critical public transparency for the state correctional system by publicly reporting  

[2:11:66,176] its findings.  

[3:0:66,197] In addition, OIG is responsible for the California Rehabilitation Oversight Board (Board) that examines CDCR's various mental  

[3:1:66,207] health, substance abuse, educational, and employment programs for inmates and parolees. The Board meets at least twice  

[3:2:66,217] annually to recommend modifications, additions, and eliminations of offender rehabilitation and treatment programs. The Board  

[3:3:66,227] also submits biannual reports to the Governor, the Legislature, and the public to convey its findings on the effectiveness of  

[3:4:66,237] treatment efforts, rehabilitation needs of offenders, gaps in offender rehabilitation services, and levels of offender participation  

[3:5:66,248] and success.

[1:0:175,43] 0555   Secretary for Environmental Protection  

[2:0:66,64] The Secretary for Environmental Protection is the cabinet level agency responsible for protecting human health and the  

[3:0:66,74] environment. California Environmental Protection Agency programs reduce greenhouse gas emissions, restore and protect  

[4:0:66,84] environmental quality, and protect public health. The Secretary coordinates the state's environmental regulatory programs and  

[4:1:66,95] ensures fair and consistent enforcement of environmental law, which safeguards the state's residents and promotes the state's  

[4:2:66,105] economic vitality. The Secretary also administers environmental justice, environmental law enforcement, emergency  

[5:0:66,115] preparedness and response, Certified Unified Program Agencies, and scientific review programs. The Secretary leads  

[6:0:66,125] greenhouse gas emission reduction and climate change activities in state government, and is responsible for coordinating the  

[6:1:66,135] implementation of Chapter 488, Statutes of 2006 (AB 32). The Secretary also centralizes coordination of California-Mexico  

[6:2:66,146] border environmental efforts.

[1:0:195,43] 0650   Office of Planning and Research  

[2:0:66,64] The Office of Planning and Research (OPR) assists the Governor and the Administration in planning, research, policy  

[2:1:66,74] development, and legislative analysis. OPR formulates long-range state goals and policies to address land use, climate  

[2:2:66,84] change, population growth and distribution, urban expansion, infrastructure development, groundwater sustainability and  

[2:3:66,95] drought response, and resource protection. OPR also maintains and updates the General Plan Guidelines, the California  

[2:4:66,105] Environmental Quality Act (CEQA) Guidelines, and operates the CEQA Clearinghouse, coordinating CEQA filings and state  

[2:5:66,115] compliance issues. OPR acts as the state's liaison to a variety of entities including local government, planning professionals,  

[2:6:66,125] small business, and the military. OPR houses and supports the Strategic Growth Council. In addition, OPR houses California  

[2:7:66,135] Volunteers, a program aimed to increase the number and impact of Californians involved with service and volunteering  

[2:8:66,146] throughout the state.

[8:0:66,589] 0360 - STATE PLANNING AND POLICY DEVELOPMENT  

[9:0:66,610] The major activities of the State Planning and Policy Development program include: (1) recommending and implementing state  

[9:1:66,620] policies on land-use and growth planning, including General Plan Guidelines; (2) coordinating the implementation of the  

[9:2:66,630] California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), including: (a) operating the State Clearinghouse; (b) maintaining an online  

[9:3:66,640] database for the electronic submission and archiving of CEQA documents; (c) developing CEQA guidelines; (d) providing  

[9:4:66,651] technical assistance; (e) streamlining permitting; and (f) providing training; (3) supporting the Governor's Advisor for Military  

[9:5:66,661] Affairs and Governor's Military Council; (4) providing policy research for the Governor and Cabinet; (5) providing technical  

[9:6:66,671] advice to, and developing guidelines for, local governments on land use planning and compliance with environmental statutes  

[9:7:66,681] and regulations; (6) developing a program to coordinate regional and local efforts with state climate adaptation strategies  

[9:8:66,691] regarding the impacts of climate change; (7) supporting drought and fire response efforts; (8) supporting development of state  

[9:9:66,702] plans including, but not limited to, the Water Action Plan, the State Multi-Hazard Mitigation Plan, the Safeguarding California  

[9:10:66,712] Plan, the Integrated Energy Policy Report, and the 5-Year Infrastructure Plan; (9) supporting multiple efforts to address climate

[3:8:66,158] 0365 - CALIFORNIA VOLUNTEERS  

[4:0:66,178] California Volunteers is charged with increasing the number and impact of Californians involved with service and volunteering  

[4:1:66,188] throughout the state. California Volunteers administers programs such as AmeriCorps and Disaster Volunteering and  

[4:2:66,199] Preparedness, and guides policy development to support the nonprofit and service fields.   Through the efforts of California  

[4:3:66,209] Volunteers, Californians of all ages and abilities are provided ongoing opportunities to volunteer, to become better prepared to  

[4:4:66,219] respond in emergencies and disasters, and ultimately, to embrace a lifetime ethic of service.  

[4:5:66,237] 0370 - STRATEGIC GROWTH COUNCIL  

[5:0:66,257] The Council coordinates interagency efforts and partners with local and regional government stakeholders to promote  

[5:1:66,267] sustainable development, improving air and water quality, protecting natural resources and agricultural lands, and reducing  

[5:2:66,277] greenhouse gas emissions. The Council administers the Transformative Climate Communities Program, the Affordable  

[5:3:66,288] Housing and Sustainable Communities Program, and the Sustainable Agricultural Lands Conservation Program, developing  

[5:4:66,298] guidelines, reviewing applications, and providing funding as part of greenhouse gas reduction efforts associated with cap and  

[5:5:66,308] trade funds. The Council also administers a technical assistance program to support all Greenhouse Gas Reduction Fund  

[5:6:66,318] programs to assist in the development of projects that maximize greenhouse gas reductions. The Council administers Climate  

[5:7:66,328] Change and Adaptation Research Program funding. The Council is charged with review of the California 5-Year Infrastructure  

[5:8:66,339] plan and with making grants and loans to institutions for planning and implementing land uses that achieve the goals of the  

[5:9:66,349] State's Planning Priorities. The Council also oversees the Health in All Policies program, and sponsors research on infill  

[5:10:66,359] development, conservation, and other planning issues.

[0:0:66,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — LJE   114   LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:202,43] 0690   Office of Emergency Services  

[2:0:66,64] The principal objective of the Office of Emergency Services (Cal OES) is to protect lives and property, build capabilities, and  

[2:1:66,74] support our communities for a resilient California. The Cal OES Director serves as both the Governor's Homeland Security  

[3:0:66,84] Advisor and Emergency Management Director, with responsibility to implement and facilitate the state's homeland security and  

[3:1:66,95] counter-terrorism strategy. The Cal OES serves the public through effective collaboration in preparing for, protecting against,  

[3:2:66,105] responding to, recovering from, and mitigating the impacts of all hazards and threats.  

[4:0:66,123] On a day-to-day basis, Cal OES provides leadership, assistance, training, and support to state and local agencies and  

[5:0:66,133] coordinates with federal agencies in planning and preparing for the most effective use of federal, state, local, and private sector  

[5:1:66,143] resources in emergencies. This emergency planning is based upon a system of mutual aid whereby a jurisdiction relies first on  

[5:2:66,153] its own resources, and then requests assistance from its neighbors. The Cal OES' plans and programs are coordinated with  

[5:3:66,163] those of the federal government, other states, private sector, utilities, and state and local agencies within California.  

[6:0:66,181] During an emergency, Cal OES functions as the Governor's immediate staff to provide guidance and coordinate the state's  

[6:1:66,191] responsibilities under the Emergency Services Act and applicable federal statutes. It also acts as the conduit for federal  

[6:2:66,201] assistance through natural disaster grants and federal agency support. Additionally, Cal OES is responsible for the  

[7:0:66,212] development and coordination of a comprehensive state strategy related to all hazards, including terrorism, which includes  

[7:1:66,222] prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery.  

[8:0:66,240] The Cal OES implements the state homeland security strategy by overseeing the State Threat Assessment System (STAS),  

[8:1:66,250] which includes management of the State Threat Assessment Center (STAC). The STAS is comprised of four regional and one  

[8:2:66,260] major urban area Fusion Centers that assist in the detection, prevention, investigation, and response to criminal and terrorist  

[8:3:66,270] activity; and information and intelligence sharing between local, state, federal, tribal agencies, and private sector partners. The  

[8:4:66,280] STAC operates as the state's information-sharing clearinghouse for strategic threat analysis, counter-terrorism, situational  

[8:5:66,291] awareness reporting, and case analysis for statewide leadership, agencies, and public safety community. Further, Cal OES  

[8:6:66,301] improves the criminal justice system in California by providing financial and technical assistance to local governments, state  

[8:7:66,311] agencies, and the private sector for homeland security, public safety, and victim services.  

[9:0:66,329] Because Cal OES' programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, Cal OES has a capital outlay program to support this  

[9:1:66,339] need. For the specifics on Cal OES' capital outlay program, see "Infrastructure Overview."

[7:0:66,247] Government Code, Title 2, Division 1, Chapter 7; Government Code, Title 5, Division 2, Part 1, Chapter 1, Article 6; Penal

[8:0:66,294] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[9:0:66,312] • California Disaster Assistance Act   — The Budget includes an augmentation of $28.8 million one-time General Fund to  

[9:1:66,322] increase the amount of funding available through the California Disaster Assistance Act (CDAA), which is used to repair,  

[9:2:66,332] restore, or replace public real property damaged or destroyed by a disaster, and to reimburse local governments for costs  

[9:3:66,343] associated with certain emergency activities undertaken in response to a state of emergency. This augmentation increases  

[9:4:66,353] total available funding for CDAA to $91.4 million in 2019-20.  

[10:0:66,371] • Public Safety Power Shutdown — The Budget includes $75 million one-time General Fund to improve resiliency of the state's  

[10:1:66,381] critical infrastructure and provide grants to communities to improve local planning and preparedness in response to utility-led  

[10:2:66,391] Public Safety Power Shutdown   events.  

[11:0:66,409] • State Emergency Telephone Number Account, Broadbrand Communication,   and 9-1-1 Advancements and   Integration   — The  

[11:1:66,419] Budget includes a $50 million one-time General Fund investment, that will be deposited into the State Emergency Telephone  

[11:2:66,429] Network Account (SETNA),   to continue implementing improvements to the state's 9-1-1 system. In addition, the Budget  

[11:3:66,439] includes $1 million ongoing SETNA to support the implementation and workload associated with emergency communications  

[11:4:66,449] coordination and First Responder Network Authority broadband network services.  

[12:0:66,467] • Disaster Preparedness, Response, and Recovery — The Budget includes $26.6 million General Fund and $900,000 Federal  

[12:1:66,477] Trust Fund (FTF)   in 2019-20 and $6.6 million General Fund   and $900,000 FTF ongoing to enhance the state's disaster  

[12:2:66,488] preparedness, response, and recovery capabilities, support the continuity of state government during disasters, and maximize  

[12:3:66,498] eligible federal reimbursements.  

[13:0:66,515] • Mutual Aid System — The Budget includes $25 million ongoing General Fund for prepositioning of existing OES and local  

[13:1:66,526] government resources that are part of the statewide mutual aid system with the goal of enhancing disaster response readiness.  

[13:2:66,536] Prepositioning occurs in areas of identified potential fire threat, which is determined through various means such as weather  

[13:3:66,546] modeling, high wind zones, low humidity, and dense fire load. The prepositioned resources include, but are not limited to, fire  

[13:4:66,556] engines, trucks, personnel, and strike teams. 

[14:0:66,574] • California Earthquake Early Warning System   — The Budget includes $16.3 million one-time General Fund to complete  

[14:1:66,584] activities associated with finishing the build-out of the California Earthquake Early Warning System, including finishing seismic  

[14:2:66,594] stations installation, adding GPS stations to the network, improving telemetry, and launching a statewide education and  

[14:3:66,605] outreach campaign. The California Earthquake Early Warning System is anticipated to be operative statewide by June 2021. 

[15:0:66,622] • California State Nonprofit Security Grant Program — The Budget includes $15 million one-time General Fund to support  

[15:1:66,632] target hardening and other physical security enhancements to assist non-profit organizations that are at high risk of a terrorist  

[15:2:66,643] attack and that have historically been targets of hate-motivied violence based on ideology and beliefs.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:167,32] 0690   Office of Emergency Services - Continued 

[3:0:66,76] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[4:0:66,94] 0380 - EMERGENCY MANAGEMENT SERVICES  

[5:0:66,115] Through the Emergency Management Services and Homeland Security Program, Cal OES provides effective use of federal,  

[5:1:66,125] state, and local resources, through the mutual aid system to jurisdictions whose resources and services are overextended in a  

[5:2:66,135] disaster situation. In addition, this program develops and coordinates a comprehensive state strategy related to an all-hazards  

[5:3:66,145] approach that includes prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery consistent with the National Strategy on  

[5:4:66,156] Homeland Security. Central to this effort is maintaining operational readiness at all levels of government. Within this program,  

[5:5:66,166] Cal OES also trains emergency managers, responders, and state and local leaders in preparedness and response to all  

[5:6:66,176] hazards.  

[6:0:66,194] 0385 - SPECIAL PROGRAMS AND GRANT MANAGEMENT  

[7:0:66,211] The Cal OES serves as the State Administering Agent for federal homeland security, emergency management, and victim  

[7:1:66,222] services grants and is the primary liaison with the Federal Emergency Management Agency, the United States Department of  

[7:2:66,232] Homeland Security, and other federal agencies. Additionally, Cal OES serves as an advocate for local public safety, emergency  

[7:3:66,242] management, and public and private agencies operating public safety and victim service programs. As part of its grant  

[7:4:66,252] management role, Cal OES administers the California Disaster Assistance Act and provides eligible local agencies with  

[7:5:66,262] financial assistance to recover from disasters. Included in this program is the State Threat Assessment Center which is the  

[7:6:66,273] centerpiece of the state's intelligence information sharing environment and serves as the Governor's designated primary fusion  

[7:7:66,283] center.  

[8:0:66,301] 0395 - PUBLIC SAFETY COMMUNICATIONS  

[9:0:66,321] Public Safety Communications (PSC) serves the State of California by providing public safety communications to the state's  

[9:1:66,331] first responders and oversight of the 9-1-1 system to the people of California. The PSC is dedicated to the preservation and  

[9:2:66,341] protection of human life and public safety by delivering reliable and dependable communication services keeping the public  

[9:3:66,352] connected during times of crisis.  

[9:4:66,369] 9900 - ADMINISTRATION AND EXECUTIVE  

[10:0:66,390] The Administration and Executive Program provides the overall policy direction of   Cal OES and support services such as  

[10:1:66,400] accounting, fiscal, personnel, information technology, and business services.

[3:0:66,78] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[4:0:66,96] The Office of Emergency Services' infrastructure includes a headquarters facility, an administrative building, and an Inland  

[4:1:66,106] Regional Operations Center in Sacramento County; a Coastal Regional Operations Center in Fairfield in Solano   County; a  

[4:2:66,116] Southern Regional Operations Center located at Los Alamitos Joint Forces Training Base in Orange County; the California  

[4:3:66,127] Specialized Training Institute at Camp San Luis Obispo; and various small field offices throughout the state.  

[5:0:66,147] The Public Safety Communications division (PSC) is comprised of a main leased complex in Sacramento and 45 field locations  

[5:1:66,157] throughout the state. These locations include 8 area offices and 37 area shops, positioned geographically to facilitate  

[5:2:66,167] maintenance and installation services to remote communication sites and customers throughout the state. In addition, the PSC  

[5:3:66,178] maintains and operates 10 communications vaults/towers and a total of more than 3,500 radio frequency points of presence.

[1:0:175,43] 0730   Governor Elect and Outgoing Governor  

[1:1:66,64] These funds are to provide assistance to the Governor-elect and the outgoing Governor, during the transition period following  

[1:2:66,74] the election, in carrying out the duties described under the provisions of Government Code Sections 12015 and 12015.5.  

[2:0:66,95] The law provides that state agencies furnish to the Governor-elect any information or assistance necessary in the preparation  

[2:1:66,105] of the annual state budget and for the orderly transfer of the executive power. This gives the Governor-elect approximately two  

[2:2:66,115] months to formulate policies and to incorporate them in the various programs included in the Budget, which will be transmitted  

[2:3:66,125] to the Legislature.  

[3:0:66,146] The law also provides for the outgoing Governor to appoint persons for up to a two-month period, to assist in concluding  

[3:1:66,156] matters arising from official duties during the last term of office.  

[4:0:66,176] Of the $1,000,000 budgeted, $865,000 is for the Governor-elect and $135,000 is for the outgoing Governor.

[0:0:66,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — LJE   136   LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:191,43] 0750   Office of the Lieutenant Governor  

[2:0:66,64] Under California's Constitution, the Lieutenant Governor serves as Acting Governor whenever the Governor is absent from the  

[2:1:66,74] state, and automatically becomes Governor if a vacancy occurs in the Office of Governor. The Lieutenant Governor is also  

[2:2:66,84] President of the Senate and votes in case of a tie.  

[3:0:66,105] The Lieutenant Governor serves as a voting member of the Board of Regents of the University of California and a voting  

[3:1:66,115] member of the Board of Trustees of the California State University system.  

[4:0:66,135] The Lieutenant Governor also serves on, and rotates with the State Controller, as chair of the three-member State Lands  

[4:1:66,146] Commission, which oversees the control and leasing of millions of acres of state-owned land, including offshore oil resources,  

[4:2:66,156] as well as use and permitting for all navigable waterways in California. The Commission also manages state land-use planning  

[4:3:66,166] and revenues, and related interstate issues. During alternate years, when the Lieutenant Governor serves as Chairperson of  

[4:4:66,176] the State Lands Commission, the Lieutenant Governor also serves as a member of the Ocean Protection Council.  

[5:0:66,197] In addition, under state statutes, the Lieutenant Governor chairs the California Commission for Economic Development, which  

[5:1:66,207] provides support and guidance to the Governor, Legislature, and private sector regarding the development of California's  

[5:2:66,217] economy.

[1:0:224,43] 0820   Department of Justice  

[2:0:66,64] The constitutional office of the Attorney General, as chief law officer of the state, has the responsibility to see that the laws of  

[3:0:66,74] California are uniformly and adequately enforced. This responsibility is fulfilled through the diverse programs of the Department  

[3:1:66,84] of Justice.  

[4:0:66,105] The Department of Justice is responsible for providing skillful and efficient legal services on behalf of the people of California.  

[4:1:66,115] The Attorney General represents the people in all matters before the Appellate and Supreme Courts of California and the  

[5:0:66,125] United States; serves as legal counsel to state officers, boards, commissioners, and departments; represents the people in  

[5:1:66,135] actions to protect the environment and to enforce consumer, antitrust, and civil laws; and assists district attorneys in the  

[6:0:66,146] administration of justice. The Department provides oversight, enforcement, education, and regulation of California's firearms/ 

[6:1:66,156] dangerous weapon laws; provides evaluation and analysis of physical evidence; regulates legal gambling activities in  

[7:0:66,166] California; supports the telecommunications and data processing needs of the California criminal justice community; and  

[7:1:66,176] pursues projects designed to protect the people of California from fraudulent, unfair, and illegal activities.

[13:2:76,515] 2019-20, $15.7 million in 2020-21, and $13.2 million in 2021-22 to continue the state’s commitment to implementing Chapter  

[13:3:76,525] 541, Statutes of 2017 (SB 384), which requires tiered registration of sex offenders.  

[13:4:65,541] • Armed Prohibited Persons (APPS) Program—The Budget includes $17.5 million ongoing General Fund for APPS—$11.9  

[13:5:76,552] million to shift the existing APPS program to the General Fund and $5.6 million to support increased APPS workload.  

[13:6:65,568] • Bureau of Forensic Services—The Budget includes $15 million one-time General Fund and $10 million one-time Fingerprint  

[13:7:76,578] Fees Account to backfill the continued decline in fine and fee revenues in the DNA Identification Fund. This funding will  

[13:8:76,588] enable the Department of Justice to continue processing forensic evidence for client counties. The Budget also includes $2.6  

[13:9:76,598] million one-time General Fund to replace critical forensic laboratory equipment.  

[13:10:65,615] • Bureau of Firearms—The Budget includes $6.9 million Dealers’ Record of Sale Account in 2019-20 and $6.4 million annually  

[13:11:76,625] thereafter to implement and enforce new laws related to firearms.  

[14:0:65,641] • Cardroom and Third-Party Provider Workload—The Budget includes $4.4 million Gambling Control Fund in 2019-20 and  

[14:1:76,651] 2020-21 to continue funding 32 positions that process license applications, renewals, and background investigations for  

[14:2:76,661] cardrooms and third-party providers to reduce the current backlog of license applications.  

[14:3:65,678] • Human Trafficking Task Forces—The Budget includes $4.2 million General Fund in 2019-20 and $3.4 million ongoing  

[14:4:76,688] thereafter to establish two task forces to address statewide human trafficking by focusing on the worst sexual predators.

[7:0:66,372] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:0:66,390] 0435 - LEGAL SERVICES  

[9:0:66,410] Legal Services is organized into three elements: (1) Civil Law, (2) Criminal Law, and (3) Public Rights.  

[10:0:66,431] Civil Law represents the State of California and its officers, agencies, departments, boards, commissions, and employees in  

[10:1:66,441] civil matters. It provides advice to these clients, defends cases brought against them and prosecutes cases to vindicate state  

[10:2:66,451] interests. Deputy Attorneys General in Civil Law are responsible for managing and litigating cases before administrative  

[10:3:66,461] tribunals, and in both state and federal courts at the trial level and on appeal, including appeals before the United States and  

[10:4:66,471] California Supreme Courts. Deputies work in one of   nine sections: Business and Tax; Cannabis Control; Correctional Law;  

[10:5:66,482] Employment and Administrative Mandates; Government Law; Health, Education and Welfare; Health Quality Enforcement;  

[10:6:66,492] Licensing; or Tort and Condemnation.  

[11:0:66,512] Criminal Law represents the   People of the State of California   in criminal matters before the Appellate and Supreme courts.  

[11:1:66,522] Criminal Law also fulfills the Attorney General's responsibilities of assisting district attorneys in cases for which they are  

[11:2:66,533] recused, conducts criminal investigations, represents the Governor, Board of Parole Hearings, and California Department of  

[11:3:66,543] Corrections and Rehabilitation in state and federal habeas corpus cases and appeals, and other proceedings relating to parole  

[11:4:66,553] decisions and conditions of confinement in the state prisons and defends criminal judgements in state and federal habeas  

[11:5:66,563] corpus matters. Additional responsibilities include enforcing the Political Reform Act, advising the Governor on extradition  

[11:6:66,573] matters, investigating and prosecuting Medi-Cal provider fraud, investigating and prosecuting the abuse or neglect of elder and  

[11:7:66,584] dependent adults residing in long-term health care facilities, and investigating, prosecuting, and coordinating litigation involving  

[11:8:66,594] white-collar crime, high-tech/computer/privacy crime, financial crimes against the elderly, human trafficking, environmental  

[11:9:66,604] crimes, and public corruption.  

[12:0:66,624] Public Rights protects and preserves the public interest by providing legal services to state agencies and Constitutional Officers  

[12:1:66,635] and by bringing actions in the name of the Attorney General to protect the public. Public Rights provides specialized services in  

[12:2:66,645] the following areas: Civil Rights Enforcement (including Underground Economy and the Bureau of Children's Justice);  

[12:3:66,655] Charitable Trusts (including the Registry of Charitable Trusts); Natural Resources Law; Corporate Fraud (including False  

[12:4:66,665] Claims, Energy and Corporate Responsibility); Indian and Gaming Law; Environment Law (including Bureau of Environmental  

[12:5:66,675] Justice); Land Law; Consumer Law (including Sellers of Travel and Privacy Enforcement and Protection); Antitrust Law; and  

[12:6:66,686] Tobacco Litigation Enforcement.  

[13:0:66,703] 0440 - LAW ENFORCEMENT  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.
